# Symbols for Logical Operators

_283 messages · 27 participants · 2003-06_

---

### Symbols for Logical Operators

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-02T16:35:37-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
Mr. Wagner has suggested that COBOL is inconsistent because it allows
symbols as synonyms for "words" used as relational operators, but does not
allow symbols as synonyms for "words" used as logical operators.

As I see it in COBOL, there are SIX classes of operators in '02-standard
COBOL:
(a) Arithmetic (+, -, *, /, **)
(b) Boolean (B-AND, B-OR, B-XOR, B-NOT)
(c) Concatenation (&)
(d) Invocation (::)
(e) Relational (GREATER etc., and *abbreviations* >, etc.)
(f) Logical (AND, OR and NOT).

Three of these are new to the language, and two of those three are special
cases with very limited context.  The other three classes of operators have
existed in the language for a very long time.

Arithmetic operators do *not* use "words", only symbols.
Relational operators use words, and may be abbreviated using symbols.
Logical operators use *only* words, not symbols.

Let's presume, for the moment, that Mr. Wagner's points about symmetry have
sufficient merit to change the standard.

If I agree to that, then I agree we need symbolic synonyms for AND, OR and
NOT.

But we also need word-oriented synonyms for the math operators to ensure
that statements of the form "COMPUTE A = B MULTIPLIED-BY C
RAISED-TO-THE-POWER-OF D DIVIDED-BY (A DECREMENTED-BY 5)" are also handled
correctly.

We need a "word-oriented" synonym for the concatenation operator, say,
"concatenated-with".

We need a "word-oriented" synonym for the invocation operator used with
inline methods (I won't hazard a suggestion for this one).

We need symbols for the Boolean operators B-AND, B-OR, B-XOR and B-NOT that
do not conflict with any other symbols in the language (most particularly
the logical operators AND, OR, and NOT).

Only then will we have consistency.  And in this case, I am unconvinced that
forcing this kind of "consistency" on COBOL provides a demonstrable
improvement.

The three "basic" forms of operators that have been with us for decades are
*consistently* described in distinctive ways:

The mathematical operators are symbols.
The logical operators are words.
The relational operators are words that may be abbreviated using symbols.

The distinction among these is consistently applied and consistently
maintained in COBOL.

My opinion is that it works the way it is, it's consistent with its own
rules, and the argument for symmetry fails.

I do not support adding new special characters to the "basic special
character" list on page 61 just for the purpose of providing synonyms for
AND, OR and NOT.  That leaves us with the option of reduplicating characters
(precedents for which include ** and == in prior standards, and >> and :: in
the most recent one).   There are all sorts of ancillary questions like
whether such symbols would need separator spaces before and after them, or
whether they would themselves be separators, to eliminate or resolve
potential ambiguities (I suspect the separator spaces would be required).

After carefully considering all this, I do not plan to support the addition
of symbols as synonyms for AND, OR and NOT in COBOL.  I believe the proposal
breaks more than it enhances.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

>The three "basic" forms of operators that have been with us for decades are

>
>The mathematical operators are symbols.

We have wordy equivalents in ADD, SUBTRACT, MULTIPLY and DIVIDE. Granted, they
are verbs rather than operators, but that seems a fine distinction.
Functionally, they do the same thing.

>The logical operators are words.

Yes.

>The relational operators are words that may be abbreviated using symbols.

Yes. 

That leaves one out of three with words only.

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-02T22:24:36-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbh4dn$sde$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net...
> "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
<snip>
> >The mathematical operators are symbols.
>
> We have wordy equivalents in ADD, SUBTRACT, MULTIPLY and DIVIDE. Granted,
they
> are verbs rather than operators, but that seems a fine distinction.
> Functionally, they do the same thing.
>

The distinction between operators and "verbs" (intitial reserved word of
statements in 2002 Standard terminology) is a CRITICAL difference to the
structure of COBOL.  You certainly are *NOT* suggesting (I hope) that there
should be new "symbols" for every "verb" within COBOL are you (in order to
have "consistancy")?

If you count "verbs" and operators as PROVIDING your desired symatry for the
mathmatecal operators, then you are introducing a VERY large set of "new"
features that have words but not symbols.

As Chuck correctly indicated WHEN IT COMES TO OPERATORS

Of the 3 "traditional types"

  - One has only symbols
  - One has only words
  - One has both symbols and words
     (and actually allows words in the symbol vaiation, e.g.
         If A is > B ...   *> which is and has always been valid

Of the 3 *new* operators in the 2002 Standard
 - 2 only have symbols (no words)
 - 1 only has words (no symbols)

        ***

If providing a COMPLETE set of both symbols and words for ALL TYPES of
operators is your "goal" - you have a lot more to do.  If you goal is simply
to fix "one" of these anomolies, then you need another justification (other
than "consistancy)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-06-03T12:15:58-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0306031115.34cd7b9c@posting.google.com>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote 

> The distinction between operators and "verbs" (intitial reserved word of
> statements in 2002 Standard terminology) is a CRITICAL difference to the
> structure of COBOL.  You certainly are *NOT* suggesting (I hope) that there
> should be new "symbols" for every "verb" within COBOL are you (in order to
> have "consistancy")?

There is also the problem (ignored, or simply not thought of by RW)
that NOT is used in many places as part of the syntax of verbs where
it is not a 'logical operator'.  NOT AT END is syntax.  'AT END' is
not a condition which could be used anywhere and inverted with NOT, it
is a label introducing a specific point in the READ statement.

Even in the relational operators the [NOT] is part of the syntax of
those and is not a separate part as the NOT logical operator is.

It took ages before RW understood that NEXT SENTENCE was syntax of the
IF and SEARCH and not an imperitive statement.  His grasp of how the
language is constructed is low.

If RW proposes symbols for 'and, or, not' as the logical operators
then there will be inconsistency where NOT AT END still requires the
word.

If RW wanted to do more than a superficial beat up then he would need
to actually do some research and propose changes separately to every
statement that has NOT as part of the syntax, and that includes the
relational operators.

Actually it is possible that his only intent to be for everyone to say
'I prefer words' and then he could propose removing '<>=' on the basis
that there is 'unanimous agreement they should not be used'.
```

One mechanism for getting features approved by J4 that has been shown
to work is to introduce them as an extension and then use their
popularity as a justification for the change.

REPLACE.  If they actually do so and find it some sort of improvement
then this can be used as part of a proposal.

Alternately if no one bothers, or chooses different characters to be
beneficial to them, then that also may influence J4.

It seems, however, that you would not find this to suit your intent. 
After failing miserably to convince us to change our code to use
words, you faked 'religeous conversion' to symbols and are attempting
to convince/coerse us to change our code to use some squiggles that no
one cares about.

It isn't about programming it is all about RW's ego and probably

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:217e491a.0306091156.33da9b52@posting.google.com...

> If people want to use symbols for and/or/not then they can do using
> REPLACE.  If they actually do so and find it some sort of improvement
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> beneficial to them, then that also may influence J4.

The problem with this is that once it's possible to use symbols via REPLACE
there's not a whole lot of motivation to change the standard to restrict the
use of the symbols to the context in question -- it's more likely that J4
would respond "We're not willing to prevent people from using these symbols
as replacements wherever, and for whatever, they wish".  Robert's been
arguing freedom of choice -- expanding the COBOL character list enhances
freedom.  Adding the symbols as standard COBOL abbreviations doesn't.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> Adding the symbols as standard COBOL abbreviations does not prevent anyone

No. What stops that is 'common sense'.

Using sqiggles d'jour to confuse current Cobol programmers and
confound everyone else (unless,possibly, they just happen to be the
exact ones they are used to) is bad enough. Reusing those to mean
something entirely different is a design for destroying all that makes
Cobol maintainable.

What is your _actual_ reason for wanting these symbols ?  Do you have
one ?

You seem to rate 'accessing API', using malloc() and doing bit-wise as
being the most important features for your needs.  Now you want to use
C (or something vaguely like C) operators.  Why do you want to
transform Cobol into C ?

Most are happy to write C programs in C and Cobol programs in Cobol.
Why do you want to reverse this ?

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >> I want Cobol to be a general purpose language .. with the capability of C
> >> but readable.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> With appropriate design, yes. 

Do you think that adding a handful of symbols that you made up will
enhance the design of the language ?

> If it's not best for a particular sub-task, for example GUI, it could call 
> the language which is .. only for the sub-task. 

So why did you want to Cobol to be a do-it-all language ?

> We wouldn't be writing the whole application in VB simply because VB is 
> good at GUI.

VB is 'Windows only', probably not a good choice anyway.

In any case, we _can_ do all that, and have been able to for a decade
or more.  In fact the problem is not with Cobol at all, it is with the
implementation(s).

In the late 60s I could write a Cobol program that CALLed Fortran and
Algol, and anything else that ran on the machine, because they all
followed the one calling and library conventions.

With MF Cobol/2 I could call MSC, Lattice C and MS-Pascal. Borland
products didn't work because _they_ didn't use the Lattice C
conventions.

Now Peter can give you a run down on exactly how to write and use
components. So instead of just whining about what the J4 should be
doing and sending trivial little cosmetic nonsense, just get on with
it and do it.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> >We already have PL/I that attempted to do that.    Will being a tool that
can
>>  do everything make it the best tool for anything?
>> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>enhance the design of the language ?

I meant application design. Cobol can be the best general-purpose tool if the
application is designed modularly .. using PECD's components or thousands of
existing components or home-grown components. Writing monolithic programs is a
lose in any language, no matter well the language is designed. 

>> If it's not best for a particular sub-task, for example GUI, it could call 
>> the language which is .. only for the sub-task. 
>
>So why did you want to Cobol to be a do-it-all language ?

Because job advertisements read like this:

Required Skills/Experience:
NATURAL2
ADABAS
CICS COBOL 
Batch COBOL 
NativeSQL
DYL280
JCL
CLISTs

The person they hire will have ALL those skills listed on his resume. There are
possibly two million Cobol programmers. How many have ALL those skills? Hundreds
to a few thousand. Why does the shop have all those languages? Because someone,
at some point in time, decided NATURAL2 was easier than Cobol + ESQL. Because
someone else, at another time, thought DYL280 was easier than Cobol for
quickies. 

As a result, I'm "not qualified" for the job, even though I _wrote_ the ancestor
of DYL280. I know nothing about NATURAL2, but how much different from PL/SQL
could it be? I'm sure I could learn it in a day or two. 

If they did everything in Cobol and SQL, they would benefit from a vastly larger
labor pool and I would benefit from being "qualified". The process of matching
people with jobs would be much more efficient. 

>> We wouldn't be writing the whole application in VB simply because VB is 
>> good at GUI.
>
>VB is 'Windows only', probably not a good choice anyway.

I agree, but millions have made that choice. 

>In any case, we _can_ do all that, and have been able to for a decade
>or more.  In fact the problem is not with Cobol at all, it is with the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>followed the one calling and library conventions.

Because compilers all came from the same hardware manufacturer. Microsoft's
Dot-NET is an effort to return to that communication standard. It's one of the
few benefits of monopolies. 

How many Cobol programmers actually used that feature? How many designed
multi-lingual applications from the ground up? Probably very few. In my
experience, Cobol programmers live in their own enclave.

>With MF Cobol/2 I could call MSC, Lattice C and MS-Pascal. Borland
>products didn't work because _they_ didn't use the Lattice C
>conventions.

If memory serves (I might be wrong), Realia could talk to Borland and most other
compilers. I used to routinely call functions in the MSC Standard library from
Realia programs. 

If the compiler doesn't provide communication, it's trivial to rephrase the call
in an assembly language 'wrapper'.  

>Now Peter can give you a run down on exactly how to write and use
>components. So instead of just whining about what the J4 should be
>doing and sending trivial little cosmetic nonsense, just get on with
>it and do it.

With the apparent exception of Peter, companies hire contractors with
yesterday's skills, not tomorrow's. A cursory search for "cobol component
programmer" on dice.com returned 20 hits, most of which used 'component' in a
non-technical sense.
```

an effort to try to hold onto monopoly.  MS only have an interest in
making a 'standard' that requires MS products and will change to
ensure that others are incompatible.
 
> How many Cobol programmers actually used that feature? How many designed
> multi-lingual applications from the ground up? Probably very few. In my
> experience, Cobol programmers live in their own enclave.

On the one hand you want Cobol to be used for everything, when
programmers have done so you call them 'living in an enclave'.

You really don't know what you want do you, except to blame everyone
else.

> If memory serves (I might be wrong), Realia could talk to Borland and most
> other compilers. 

Borland, and others, tends to be incompatible with anything that can
call MSC.  This is because of different 'start ups', and especially
problems with DLLs.

> I used to routinely call functions in the MSC Standard library from
> Realia programs. 

Exactly, so what was your point again ?
 
> If the compiler doesn't provide communication, it's trivial to rephrase the
>  call in an assembly language 'wrapper'.  

Exactly, so what was you point again ?

> With the apparent exception of Peter, companies hire contractors with
> yesterday's skills, not tomorrow's. 

That is the way of the world.  The existing staff get the new
developments and new toys to play with. 'Legacy' gets done from
outside.

If they hired new staff to do new work and left all the existing junk
with current staff they would all leave,
You aren't going to change that.

riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

Willingness to learn and 'book knowledge' count for nothing in the US. The first
screener, the recruiter, wants verifiable recent experience. He wants the phone
number of a reference who will say 'Yes, RW did NATURAL2 for one year.' 

Recruiters don't judge applicants' talent or intelligence. They mechanically
match requirements to experience. If one requirement, no matter how
insignificant, is missing, the application is rejected. 

>> Why does the shop have all those languages? Because someone,
>> at some point in time, decided NATURAL2 was easier than Cobol + ESQL. Because
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

>Then get to it.  You won't get a job by complaining that it is
>everyone else's fault.

Neither will I get that job by learning NATURAL2. The only acceptable evidence
of knowledge is verifiable experience. If I worked in a shop that used it, I
'have' the skill. If I developed world-class expertise on nights and weekends, I
do not 'have' the skill because it's not verifiable. 

That's how the hiring process works in the US. Every language on the
requirements list is a potential rejection factor. 

>> If they did everything in Cobol and SQL, they would benefit from a vastly
larger
>> labor pool and I would benefit from being "qualified". The process of

>> people with jobs would be much more efficient. 
>
>So instead of pissing around with cosmetic trivia why [aren't] you learning

Because there's no demand for '02 Cobol skills. Demand is vendor specific. 

"Do you know Micro Focus Cobol?"
"Cobol is an ANSI standard language. I've been doing it 40 years."
"How many of those years were on [raising voice for emphasis] MICRO FOCUS?"
"Umm, at least five. Before that I did Realia for fifteen years."
"The client doesn't have Realia. Give me the phone number of someone who can
verify your MICRO FOCUS experience."

>> I agree, but millions have made that choice.

the
>> few benefits of monopolies. 
>

should be done in product X'.  The incentive is to get product X on
the CV.  Then the next thing gets done in product Y.

I get on my CV'.  You are complaining that others have been able to

be used with other languages.

much would it take to squash that when MS wants to ?
  
What MS wants to eliminate is J2EE which can run on anything and thus
doesn't require Windows.

not the best way to advance the state of the art. 

>> That's how the hiring process works in the US. Every language on the
>> requirements list is a potential rejection factor. 
>
>Is this all about blaming someone else that you can't get a job ?

Before the recent slowdown, I could find a gig in less than a month. Now it
takes 3-6 months. I haven't changed; the marketplace has changed. 

>> Good alternatives are Flexus and MS C++. Both offer a real programming
>> language. 
>
>C++ language doesn't have a GUI, there are some MFC classes usable by
>C++ that provide a thin layer over Windows.

VC++ 6.0 and above does provide a GUI as good as VB. Granted, it's done by calls
to OLE, but you can drag and drop widgets as easily as you can with VB. 

>Excuse me, but what does the Flexus 'programming language' look like ?
>
>While SP/2 may be targetted at Cobol, there is no reason why it can't
>be used with other languages.

Good point. I'd thought of Flexus as an extension to Cobol. 

>> I don't see how they can make others incompatible. It's an open standard.
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>doesn't require Windows.

.NET runs similar JIT compiled pseudo-code. How hard would it be to compile it
under Unix or another operating system?

>> My current project terminated unexpectedly. As a job seeker I need high
>> self-esteem. CLC, your postings in particular, work contrary to that

Richard:

In fact, you are absolutely correct.

Our first release of our screen handling software back in 1986
(although not GUI) supported many different programming languages,
including C, Pascal and Assembler.  In 1987, we decided to focus
primarily on COBOL, because that was the language for which we were
selling the vast majority of our software.

In addition, our panel editor is written in C, yet it invokes calls to
sp2 to perform it's own GUI screen handling.  Therefore, we are using
sp2 in a C language program.

You are 100% correct.

Robert,

Having read this and the posts that followed, and being determined that
everyone should get a fair deal <G>, I spent the last hour or so searching
Google Groups, looking through your previous posts.

Richard says your stated purpose in coming here was to "rub our noses in
it". I can't find anything you posted that contains words to that effect.
(Google currently has over 1900 posts which it claims you authored)... It
may be my search was flawed, but I'm prepared to give you the benefit of the
doubt on that one.

Don points out that you don't know a joke or how to take it. I was shocked
when I saw your response to his post on "the obscene clone falls" (I liked
Don's version better anyway, but for you to claim his post was "wrong" just
makes his point...)

Bill has persevered with you against all his better inclinations.

The Doc has been his usual "pedantic but correct" self, treating you no
worse or better than  he treats anybody else here... <G>.

For myself, I have striven to maintain neutrality and cut you as much slack
as possible, even when your posts have been insulting and outrageous. I kind
of hoped things would settle and you would pick up the tone of the group and
they, in turn would understand your personality a bit better. I see this as
being a good outcome for all concerned.

It hasn't happened.

Instead you have decided that the regulars here are  a 'bunch of grouchy old
men preserving the status quo of
mediocre programming, and covering it with a social compact 'There is no
such thing as good or bad programming; it's all a matter of style." ',  who
couldn't possibly know as much about computing in general, and COBOL in
particular, as you do.

Yet, these same "grouchy old men" have responded to your posts, tried to
have reasoned arguments with you (for the most part, not always...<G>) and
NOT simply blocked your post or ignored your points.

If you were honest you would admit that some of the interactions here made
you re-think your position on a number of things and may even have expanded
your knowledge. You are certainly no poorer for having been here.

Looking back through your posts, there is a common thread of self pity. Yet
most of your wounds are self-inflicted.

I re-read your original "Best Practices" and I can see merit in a lot of it.
There is no objection in principle to someone coming newly to COBOL
benefitting from the tried and true practices of an old hand, and even
cutting through much of the "extraneous" material to get them flying
quickly. (But there are some necessary provisos to this approach which you
never even entertained... ). The main problem is in the way you have
presented it. It reads as Holy Writ delivered in a supercilious tone that
says "The rest of them are too stupid to see that most of this stuff is
unnecessary, but stick with me and you'll be all right.".  There is no
attempt to recognise that there are many ways in COBOL to achieve results
and that there are many possible styles, none of which is intrinsically
"wrong". (But you are unable to see such shades of grey...). Such an
approach was bound to provoke negative reaction from a group such as this,
or indeed, from any reasonable COBOL programmer with more than 5 years
experience, who has realised the subtleties of style and learned to
differentiate between what IS important and what ISN'T.

As for not having a job, you genuinely have my sympathy. I hate to see any
contractor in that situation unless it is by choice.

You are a very competent and experienced programmer. If you let agencies
stop you because you haven't got Bendix autocode or whatever bullshit tech
requirement is flavour of the Month this month, then you are not the man you
think you are. (WE know that, but you don't, and certainly the Agents
don't...<G>). Seriously, you must have contacts you could approach based on
your track record? Try the direct approach. What can you lose?

Making COBOL into a "one language wonder" won't solve this problem. The
Market has changed. (And the change is not over yet either...) The only
defence is to maintain and expand the skill set. The ultimate solution is to
get out of programming as a career, but that is beyond the scope of this
post.

Given that you included me in the "grouchy old men" group, I won't be
responding to further posts from you.

Nevertheless, I wish you success with the job hunting.

Pete.
```

```
"Don Leahy" <don.leahy@nospamwhatever.leacom.ca> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:3eeec710.213605506@news.optonline.net...
…[44 more quoted lines elided]…
>RW, I guess it's been a while since you've worked on a mainframe eh? 

Not so long -- 2-3 years on HUGE iron (IBM Global Services, Merrill Lynch).

>I know
>this because in an earlier post you mentioned that the target response time
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>for most applications).

You're right. I remembered 2 seconds from the '80s. Modern mainframes were
faster, but not as fast as Big Unix (HP) or even Small Unix on the Sun Solaris
line. The speed of a 'desktop-class' Sun Ultra running as server is very
impressive. 

>Do you think that there might be a *reason* why mainfamers are conservative
>in their approach to design?

It goes to the conflict between evolution and revolution. An evolutionist wants
to change one variable at a time and make sure it works before going to the
next. A revolutionist wants to discard the past and design from the ground up. 

Evolution is inherently more reliable. The problem is it can only handle input
challenges at its own pace. If the world is changing faster, it's doomed. Look
at the dinosaurs. They became extinct (or morphed into birds) because they
couldn't adapt to changing climate quickly enough. 

In the computer world there is also the baggage of backward compatibility. You
cannot delete anything for fear of 'breaking' legacy code. Our DNA has the same
problem, which explains why 90% of it is non-functional. We carry the
instructions for microbes and fish, which no longer apply. 

The source language can bridge the gap. If Cobol compiles and runs the same on a
Unix or NT box, which it does, we're no longer tied to mainframe technology.
I've moved hundreds of programs off mainframe with little trouble. Controls and
backup were comparable. The only differences were lower cost and a more
liberated mentality.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 27)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-06-23T19:28:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ef753ac.46942538@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <217e491a.0306142107.2808246@posting.google.com> <3ef274e3.119012751@news.optonline.net> <bcuui1$o5d$1@panix1.panix.com> <3ef37a65.26614716@news.optonline.net> <bd75es$6ki$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>  I guess it is to make us just like you, but my bosses would need to know what
>is in it for them.

I ran the IT department at a West Texas supermarket company from 1980 to
mid-1986 using the ideas I present here. During that time our total IT budget
was $1M per year. That covered hardware (a small IBM mainframe), IBM software,
supplies, keypunch operators, computer operators, six programmers and me. 

The company's sales varied between $1B and $2B (the higher figure after we
acquired Safeway's El Paso division), the number of stores was 100-200 (mostly
scanning, hosted by my department), employees: 12,000-20,000. Published industry
averages say a medium-sized supermarket company's IT budget should be half of
one percent of sales. Thus,  we should have been spending $5-10M. Our
competitors did spend that much. HEB (San Antonio), with similar stores and
revenues, had 60 programmers and two large MVS mainframes. 

What's in it for your bosses is $4-9M on the bottom line.

You might think they got sub-standard computer service for that low price.
Actually, it was better than most. We price checked every line item of every
Direct Store Delivery (DSD) invoice, about 30K invoices per week, in which we
detected overcharges of $1M per year. I was unaware of any other supermarket
company which did that. Our produce warehouse turnover was 120% per day. Much of
it went directly from receiving dock to shipping dock without hitting a shelf
(all controlled by computer).  My warehouse system was so good that physical
count was done only once a year. On a $40M inventory, the error was usually less
than $1K. That's .0025%

After I left, they hired a 'typical mainframer' to run the shop. He deemed
everything I'd done "unprofessional", replaced the little mainframe with Big
Iron, replaced my accounting systems with ERP, hired 20 more programmers (my six
proteges became team leads), hired contractors galore, etc. ad nauseum. The IT
budget went from $1M to $7M, response time went down the drain, they stopped
price checking DSD invoices because CICS was too slow, a year later the
accounting systems were three months behind. 

Show this posting to your CFO, not empire-building middle managers, and ask for
his opinion.
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On 20-Jun-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>How does CPU speed relate to "work done"?

We get work done by running programs. If you move 10,000 Cobol programs from a
16-processor mainframe to a 16-processor Unix machine, they'll do the same work
in about the same time. 

In physics, work is the product of force, displacement and the angle between the
two (theta). In computing, CPU speed is the force, Cobol programs are the theta
and rearranged bits are the displacement. Since the latter two are the same, the
amount of work done is a function of CPU speed.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 29)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-06-24T09:25:43+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bd7r92$g9v$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <bcpr5i$pag$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3ef75471.47139719@news.optonline.net> <bd7m14$e26$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```

> On 23-Jun-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
> 
>> >> CPUs run at 1-3 GHz.  If the big blue box had 16 of them, a red box
>> >> with 16 will run just as fast.

This is not true at all in any way.  MHz/GHz is not a measure of how many 
instructions are done per second, but is only of the clock speed of the CPU 
cycles. How many cycles each instruction takes, how much data is worked on 
and how much overlap can exist depends on the type of CPU and these vary 
enormously.

Just because a Honda 50cc motorcycle's engines can do 22,000 rpm does not 
make it 3 times as fast as a family car.

>> We get work done by running programs. If you move 10,000 Cobol programs
>> from a 16-processor mainframe to a 16-processor Unix machine, they'll do
>> the same work in about the same time.

This is not true in any way. The amount of work done for business 
applications is more dependant on the data throughput than it is on the CPU 
speed.  

Just because a car has 8 cylinders does not mean that it will be as fast as 
an F1, or have the carrying capacity of a Mack truck even though these too 
have 8 cylinders.

>> In physics, work is the product of force, displacement and the angle
>> between the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> same, the amount of work done is a function of CPU speed.

Pseudo-intellectual babble doesn't make your argument any better.

The 'rearranged bits' are on the disk files, screens and printers and these 
have no necessary direct relationship to 'CPU speed'.

As is quite often the case you have taken an overly simplistic view, a 
superficial one, presumably because any deeper evaluation will show you to 
be wrong.
```

```
>What red boxes have the capabilties of IBM FICON channels?

FICON runs at 2Gb/s. Fiber Channel, which runs at the same speed, is readily
available and a bit less expensive.  If 2Gb isn't fast enough, you can run two
Fiber Channels in parallel. See for example qlogic.com, whose channel controller
sells for $500. 

For drives, the Seagate Cheetah provides 73GB for about $700. It runs at 15000
rpm, same as the fastest IBM drive. Can you buy an IBM drive for $700 .. hmm?

><snip>

>>

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0306191204.614ed96c@posting.google.com>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >> Because modular design and multi-language are not the norm in COBOL shops,
> >> especially mainframe shops.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> single module program. 

You are taking a very narrow minded view of 'single-module'.

For example your 'definition' uses the term 'main program'. In a
Fujitsu Cobol application one program must be marked as 'main' and all
others will be called. In MicroFocus the same structure usually
applies but it is only implicit as to which is the 'main'.

In IBM mainframes (according to discussions here) there is no such
distinction about 'main'.

Opening files in a program doesn't stop it being modular, that is just
an arbitrary opinion that you have.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: RW and <IBM> Mainframe environments (was: Symbols for Logical Operators

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: RW and <IBM> Mainframe environments (was: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 26)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-06-20T18:21:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0306201721.4d0a56d4@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <217e491a.0306142107.2808246@posting.google.com> <3eec8e82.68033643@news.optonline.net> <3eedae7f_4@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3eeec710.213605506@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306171538.48bd0c0e@posting.google.com> <3ef04322.310918563@news.optonline.net> <bcq2ok$jtc$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3ef0e38b.16252934@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306191204.614ed96c@posting.google.com> <3ef250df.109790989@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> You're hung up on Fujitsu's linker-oriented definition of Main. It's a
> technical detail, get over it.

Not at all, I discussed how some systems used the term 'main' in a
specific way and _also_ how mainframes did not use the term in any
significant way.

You had used the term 'main' without qualifying what you meant by it.

> In my view, if a Main program accesses files, the whole structure is
> upside-down. File access should be at the bottom of the structure, not at the
> top. At the top should be business logic.

That may be your view.  Others may have other views.  In the earliest
forms of Cobol that I used (ANSI '61 based) I had to access disk files
using CALLs to the systems DA Housekeeping.  Putting FDs, OPENs and
READs into the programming was considered an advance.  You are
advocating returning to what I did 35 years ago.

Now there may be some advantages to isolating the disk accesses into a
separate module for each file.  For example if the file changes in
some ways, such as new fields being added, then it may be possible to
not recompile programs which do not use those fields.  This does,
however require some care as the additional size of the linkage area
may overwrite programs that have the original shorter area.

There are other ways of catering for these types of need _without_
recompiling. Whether the file module approach is, in fact, an
advantage depends entirely on what problems arise and what solutions
are found.
Now I do have file access modules for most of the files in my systems.
 My Report Writer program set can, from scripts and dictionares,
produce reports on any file for which a module exists.  The module is
loaded dynamically getting its name from the script.

However, whether the module is used by other programs, or the file
access is done directly depends entirely on which is the most
advantageous.

As you cannot possibly know the criteria by which I choose, nor
predict what the result of these criteria is for any particular
situation then I deny you the facility of requiring that I do it in
some particular way.

But let us look at your particular claim:

   'File access should be at the bottom of the structure'

Many programs will 'access files', that is not what you actually mean.
 You are merely trying to make a distiction between 'READ file' and
'CALL "filemodule" USING record-area, file-status' or similar.

On MF you could make those CALLs directly to EXTFH, how would _that_
make it 'modular' ?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: RW and <IBM> Mainframe environments (was: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 27)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-06-21T19:15:23+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ef48946.16233690@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <217e491a.0306142107.2808246@posting.google.com> <3eec8e82.68033643@news.optonline.net> <3eedae7f_4@corp-news.newsgroups.com> <3eeec710.213605506@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306171538.48bd0c0e@posting.google.com> <3ef04322.310918563@news.optonline.net> <bcq2ok$jtc$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3ef0e38b.16252934@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306191204.614ed96c@posting.google.com> <3ef250df.109790989@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306201721.4d0a56d4@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>significant way.

I agree -- there should not be a 'main' designation on an executable. 

>You had used the term 'main' without qualifying what you meant by it.

Most programmers understand 'main' to mean the program executed first.

>> In my view, if a Main program accesses files, the whole structure is
>> upside-down. File access should be at the bottom of the structure, not at the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>advocating returning to what I did 35 years ago.

As I recall, Cobol had sequential files from the beginning. Perhaps you are
thinking of ISAM files, which were introduced in 68.

>Now there may be some advantages to isolating the disk accesses into a
>separate module for each file.  For example if the file changes in
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>may overwrite programs that have the original shorter area.

The calling program sends a version parameter that tells the IO module the
record format. There is no need for filler or recompile. 

The file may have been completely redesigned or moved to a database. 

>Now I do have file access modules for most of the files in my systems.
> My Report Writer program set can, from scripts and dictionares,
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>make it 'modular' ?

No, it wouldn't. When I design program structure, I try to organize it like a
company. The CEO at the top makes high-level decisions and delegates tasks to
middle-management, where most decisions are made. The lowest level opens files
and pulls records. Data flows upward rather than downward. The top guy never
sees raw data, only verified summaries. 

For real-time applications, I use the network model, in which departments pass
messages (emails) to each other in response to events and queries. The internal
organization of departments is still hierocratic, with managers making decisions
and delegating work to file clerks and other grunts.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What may we conclude? (was: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 26)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-23T08:44:46-07:00

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ What do you want from COBOL? (was: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-12T14:49:28-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bcalgc$cjh$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <bbh4dn$sde$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <217e491a.0306031115.34cd7b9c@posting.google.com> <3ee213ed.112456295@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306081218.3545ea52@posting.google.com> <3ee3a088.214002499@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306091156.33da9b52@posting.google.com> <bc2ro0$1o72$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ee7b51e.35012541@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306112216.24eb9e73@posting.google.com> <3ee855be.76138248@news.optonline.net> <k24Ga.2863$3o3.235717@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
"Harley" <dennis.harleyNoSpam@worldnet.att.net> wrote in message
news:k24Ga.2863$3o3.235717@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net...
>
> "Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
> news:3ee855be.76138248@news.optonline.net...
> | riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
> |
<snip>
> If COBOL is more readable, why are proposing a change that will make it
less
> readable?
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

My personal "goal"  for relevant changes to COBOL (or Cobol or even CoBOL
<G>) would be that

A) For environments with "API's" (e.g. Windows, graphics, etc) that COBOL
provide all the facilities needed to access and use "independently provided"
API's (with portable source code)

B) For environments with Classes/Objects/Methods, COBOL provide all the
facilities to inherit from, expand, and provide base for inheritance of
"independently provided" Class/Objects/Methods

  ***

I was still working for Micro Focus when I first heard the term
"C-ification" (of COBOL).  Although not documented, I also heard this term
used in the development of the 2002 COBOL Standard.  I did not then (and do
not now) think of this as making COBOL "more C-like" - but rather as making
COBOL a "viable" option for accessing all API's that were written with C (or
C-like) "structures" and "access" in mind.  With the relative rise in Java,
C++, C#, etc - I would add to this that I think that the current (2002)
COBOL standard provides many (but probably still not ALL) the necessary
features to allow those languages to inherit from COBOL Classes and vice
versa.

Some (new in the 2002 Standard, but already extensions in many compilers)
features related to this, include:

 - RETURNING
 - By VALUE
 - USAGE Pointer and Procedure-Pointer
 - Recursion
 - OO "itself"
 - Typedefs and prototypes
 - "true binary" and floating-point USAGEs

        ***

Therefore, it is not my PERSONAL goal that COBOL be a "general purpose"
language - or even that it always be the "right tool" for *ALL* programming
jobs - it is simply that COBOL be a valid *option* for those "business"
programming tasks that are currently being done in IT.
```

The changes to COBOL have made it possible for me to write just about
anything I need in Cobol.
This included writing a code generator for optimizing updates to a DB2
system, and a small system to process APF/DS datasets. Neither could be
considered to be "Business Systems".

(An AFP/DS dataset is basicly a formatted (fancy) print dataset. Your
utility bills, bank statements, etc are probably AFP/DS. The records are
variable length, and contain formatting and positioning information as well
as the data to be printed. If you are on the mainframe these is a utility
program, ACIF, that will convert a line print file to AFP/DS format)

I find the discusion of what do you want in COBOL short sighted.

While the gradual growth of the language have been steps forward (as a rule)
other ways to grow the language are ignored for reasons beyond me. As I've
said
before, I'm slow.

The general outline of a source program written in COBOL is not much unlike
any "how to". The name of the program gives it a grouping that IMHO is far
from complete.

The configuration section establishes the hardware environ.

The data section describes the fields and files needed.Major extensions are
sometimes
 made by introducing ONE word (such as sort or compute.)

Some data attributes are defined, but far from those needed now  and again
IMHO.

Instead, the problems of such needs are "solved" in other venues creating
multiple
sets of rules, and extending the funtions of COBOL via adoption rather than
creation
of additions to the outline of COBOL.

I can see that this has been a gift from beyond for many, but has placed
COBOL
in the position of being enadequate. One program description is an objective
that is
worthy of the skills of this group. Yes, some existing tools from outside
will disappear,
but the single objective may be reached.

Surely, my position is poorly stated for some, but I believe between the
group of
you, serious work can be accomplished to reduce the tower further.

Warren Simmons

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ee7b51e.35012541@news.optonline.net...
> "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> >The problem with this is that once it's possible to use symbols via
REPLACE
> >there's not a whole lot of motivation to change the standard to restrict
the
> >use of the symbols to the context in question -- it's more likely that J4
> >would respond "We're not willing to prevent people from using these
symbols
> >as replacements wherever, and for whatever, they wish".  Robert's been
> >arguing freedom of choice -- expanding the COBOL character list enhances
> >freedom.  Adding the symbols as standard COBOL abbreviations doesn't.
>
> Adding the symbols as standard COBOL abbreviations does not prevent anyone
from
> using them alternatively, via REPLACE.

Yes, it does, because it requires THEM to use REPLACE to use them
alternatively.  I suspect you might not understand all the ramifications of
"reserved words" in COBOL.

You haven't convinced me that your proposal to reserve "^" or "/\" as a
replacement for AND is in any way different from somebody else's right to
use "^" or "/\" as a synonym for "**".  You also haven't convinced me that
the standard should enshrine the first as somehow so meritorious that a
REPLACE would nevermore be required to accomplish it, while relegating the
latter to the "well, if you really want to ..." category.

In my opinion, *either* substitution should require programmer action;
*neither* set of abbreviations should be part of the COBOL standard.
Presuming the definition of pseudo-text-1 is enhanced, any user that wants
to use either one would be free to do so.

I don't think implicitly assuming that "^" is an abbreviation for "AND"
anywhere it appears in a COBOL program is somehow more justifiable and
represents a greater improvement to the standard than implicitly assuming
that "^" is an abbreviation for "**".   I don't think implicitly assuming
*either one* is an improvement, for that matter.

       -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-09T09:45:42-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bc2djm$1e77$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <bbh4dn$sde$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <217e491a.0306031115.34cd7b9c@posting.google.com> <3ee213ed.112456295@news.optonline.net>`

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ee213ed.112456295@news.optonline.net...

> I could get everyone to say !|^ are available via REPLACE, as I
demonstrated
> they are on Micro Focus,

You could, but as Bill pointed out you'd be wrong, and you would have misled
everyone you got to say it, as a matter of standard COBOL.  Failure to check
to ensure that the contents of the "COBOL words" being replaced were
comprised of characters from within the COBOL character set is technically
nonconformant.  No implementation is obligated to allow the replace, though
some -- apparently including MicroFocus and Unisys MCP/AS COBOL85  -- do; I
am unaware of others.

> then ask why the same argument doesn't apply to =><.

You could, and as it happens the same argument you *want* to apply to ^!|
*does* apply to =><, because the latter group *is* in the COBOL character
set.  You *can* do this with REPLACE today.  The difference is that REPLACE
was introduced to the standard in '85, pseudo-text in general in '74, COPY
itself (as best I can tell) in '68.  All of these *post-date* the
introduction of the symbols =<>, which appear to have been present from the
very beginning.   Yes, you can do a REPLACE ===== by ==equal== today.  The
problem is that you couldn't until REPLACE was introduced.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

> Both REPLACE and the diagraphs '<=' and '>=' were introduced in '85. Why

I don't know; I wasn't there.  I've done what I can to rectify that omission
by suiting up and showing up for thirty months or so now.  I've invited you
on at least one occasion to do the same, but all evidence I've seen leads me
to believe it's much more in keeping with your basic character to sit on the
sidelines, complain about how the committee isn't doing things Your Way, and
basically throw rotten tomatoes at those who are actually doing the work!

I don't have ready access to the various drafts that led up to the '85
standard, and so I can't say which came first, or whether the relationship
between REPLACE and the introduction of the digraphs was discussed.  Perhaps
you can explore that with those people who *were* there in the period
between 1974 and 1985.

I've already gone on record as finding these digraphs distasteful.  The
committee's already gone on record that there were some problems in the
syntax rules as they were put forth in the '85 standard.  My personal
opinion is that the standard, and the language, would have been better off
without the digraphs as formal abbreviations, and then the responsibility
for observing the rules about NOT in the context of the digraphs would fall
on the shoulders of the person who used them via REPLACE, where I think it
belongs, not on the implementor of the language.

Personally, I think a syntax error like "SYMBOL DELETED -----> NOT" is
entirely suitable (and adequately diagnostic) for a fragment like "IF
A!<=B".

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Why (20 years ago)? (was: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-10T13:29:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bc581p$aat$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <bbh4dn$sde$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <217e491a.0306031115.34cd7b9c@posting.google.com> <3ee213ed.112456295@news.optonline.net> <bc2djm$1e77$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ee5a78d.126291827@news.optonline.net>`

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ee5a78d.126291827@news.optonline.net...
> "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
>
> >"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
> >news:3ee213ed.112456295@news.optonline.net...
>
<snip>
>
> Both REPLACE and the diagraphs '<=' and '>=' were introduced in '85. Why
were
> the diagraphs added if, as you say, they could have been specified by the
user
> via REPLACE?

J4 and WG4 usually "ignore" "why did you do this in the past" questions, and
I was *not* a part of the Standards group that developed the '85 Standard
(in the early 80's).  However, I can take a GUESS at the answer to this.

The relational operators "is GREATER than OR EQUAL to" and "is LESS than OR
EQUAL to" were added to the '85 Standard as there was then (as is still
expressed by some - not all) a desire to "avoid" the REQUIREMENT to use
"negative" tests. Therefore, even though "NOT LESS" and "NOT GREATER" were
previously available, these "new" (20 years ago) relational operators were
thought to provide a functionality that "sufficient" real-world programmers
would want, that they were added.  At that time (and in fact still today)
each set of relational operators had BOTH a "word" form and a "symbol" form.
It was felt (I would guess) that it was appropriate to continue the
"paradigm" that was already in effect.

Although CHANGING "paradigm" to *add* consistency is usually (not always)
considered insufficient reason to change the standard COBOL specification,
it is also true that when there is a perceived desire for a new feature, it
is usually (not always) added in a manner that is "consistent" with existing
features.

It is my PERCEPTION that J4/WG4 rarely add "features" *SIMPLY* to provide
additional options for existing functionality (as in their current negative
reaction to adding symbols for logical operators).  However, I believe that
they do TRY an provide new functionality in as "consistent" a manner with
previous functionality as possible (in most cases).
```

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> Both REPLACE and the diagraphs '<=' and '>=' were introduced in '85. Why were
> the diagraphs added if, as you say, they could have been specified by the user
> via REPLACE?

New features in the language are often added by many submitting
proposals each to add just one feature.

It is quite possible that the proposer of '>=' and '<=' was unaware of
the proposal to add REPLACE and vice versa.  Each proposal was taken
on its own merits.  If REPLACE had been added earlier, or was assured
as being available and as a viable way of using the symbols then
perhaps the proposer of those symbols may have withdrawn or modified
the proposal.

Your comment is based on hindsight which is less useful than you
assume.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-03T16:19:19-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbj3ff$bq6$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306022217.680b321@posting.google.com> <eimpdvooaq4d6o4biogclr1p99cqo6hvqb@4ax.com>`

```
FYI - I just sent the following note to J4 and WG4.

"A point has just come up in comp.lang.cobol that has changed my opinion on
symbols for the logical operators AND, OR, and NOT.

First,
    I remain in favor of <> for "not equal" - and don't think that it needs
to be limited to "implied" higher/lower (orderable) relational tests.

Now, for the "new" opinion.
    I now think it would be a BAD idea to add a symbol for the "NOT" logical
operator (and also the NOT reserved word in simple relation operators).

comp.lang.cobol pointed out to "us" something that I hadn't thought about.
If we allow a SYMBOL for "NOT" when it is an operator (or part of an
operator) it would be INCONSISTENT with the "NOT" in conditional phrases,
e.g. NOT AT END, NOT ON EXCEPTION. I agree with some of the posts in
comp.lang.cobol that allowing a symbol for NOT in some cases but not in
others would be highly error prone and quite user-unfriendly.

If we do not provide a symbol for NOT, then (obviously?) we shouldn't for OR
and AND.
Obviously, this is just my opinion. (I don't think there was overwhelming
support for the original idea anyway - but did think that I should present
my "change of stance" and reason for it.)"
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "John H. Lindsay" <jlindsay@kingston.net>
- **Date:** 2003-06-04T11:43:38-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3EDE13AA.66B53F0@kingston.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <01c329f9$9f7a6520$64ccf943@chottel> <bbiqfd$2j0g$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <01c32a2e$d74b3bc0$f1c2f943@chottel>`

```
Hi folks:

There's a big controversy going on here about the use of 
'operator' symbols in COBOL.  I'd like to offer my thoughts on 
the matter.  I'm a retired mathematician and computer scientist 
and hence come from a very different background from most COBOL 
programmers, although I have written a number of COBOL 
programmes from medium-sized to medium-large, definetely not 
the huge ones like the monsters that process the Canadian 
income tax submissions we file once a year.  My present 
interest in COBOL centres on the type of data it processes 
(including its binary machine format in a variety of hardware) 
and the operations done on it.  I have not read this entire 
thread, so I hope I'm not duplicating material here.

Mathematical notation for essentially all branches of the huge 
subject area is extremely terse, and of necessity so.  Without 
the abbreviation implicit in the use of symbols, writing down 
equations and other statements expressing relations between 
mathematical objects would be too slow and awkward.  Just try 
to take a good ol' high school Euclidean geometry text, pick 
almost any theorem, and try to write out the proof in words 
as opposed to using the small amount of geometrical symbolism.  
Users of any of the APL programming systems will recognize 
the usefulness and enabling nature of terse -- even though 
'wierd' -- notation.  

It is easy and necessary for a (research) mathematician to 
invent new operations in his[1] work, and then easy and 
necessary for him to invent symbols to use to write down the 
properties of and uses of his new operations.  But there are 
some operations which are useful to programmers and exist in 
computers, which have at best grudging representation in both 
English and COBOL, the 'exclusive or' logical operator and 
string concatenation of more than two varying length strings 
being cases in point.

COBOL was designed with English in mind, to a much greater 
extent than any of the Algols, Pascal, PL/I &c.; these just 
use English as a source of key words or for a very few phrases.  
Plainly, English has its limitations and points at which it 
becomes awkward and ambiguous to use even when discussing 
accounting proceedures and problems.  The original COBOL was 
useful and adequate at first -- in the 60's -- but since then, 
the language syntax and associated semantics have had to be 
be refined a number of times to deal with ambiguities, to 
recognize and implement needed operations (which existed in 
computers or as ideas, but not yet in COBOL), and to introduce 
new operations into the language.  

We are now at the point where two streams of thought about 
further development of COBOL are meeting head-on, and some 
conflict is inevitable.  On the one side, we have those who 
wish to retain the roots in English, even in the cases where 
this makes a bit of a programme wordy, awkward, difficult to 
read, or even (seemingly) ambiguous.  There is a point here 
to be taken account of: to some, even many, COBOL programmers, 
(mathematically originating) symbolism is inscrutable, not 
expressive, and confusing.  Hence to them, the introduction 
of symbolism would be a source of frustration and (much worse) 
programming errors.  On the other side, we are faced with the
pressures to introduce some new operations (which have existing 
symbols but poor English words) for useful machine data types, 
data organizations and machine data representations, and with 
the consequent pressure to retrofit symbolism into the existing 
COBOL language for existing operations.  

In a worst case scenario, we could be faced with a great 
division here, with one stream of COBOL language, programs and 
programmers going one way and another stream going another.  
This sort of thing is not easy to resolve, but I wish the 
COBOL community the best in doing so.  

John H. Lindsay 

[1]  I have followed the time-honoured English practise of 
using the masculine form of the pronouns where the antecedent 
could be, equally, either masculine or feminine.  Where 
appropriate, for he, him or his, read she, her or hers.  >
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>

```
"John H. Lindsay" <jlindsay@kingston.net> wrote 

Your input is welcome and provides a useful focus on context.

> We are now at the point where two streams of thought about 
> further development of COBOL are meeting head-on, and some 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL language for existing operations.  

While you have presented two sides to the argument, the argument is
rather more complicated than that, or certainly different.

Robert is not arguing specifically for symbols, he is arguing for
extremism. He wants the use of logical operators and comparison
operators to be "all words" or "all symbols".  Or more specifically he
wants _us_ to use 'all words', but his fallback position, based on
most saying they thought 'IS GREATER THAN OR EQUAL' to be rather
wordier than they liked, is either to get many to say 'I don't like
symbols' so that he can conclude they should be using all words, or to
accept that RW is some sort of new mesiah leading Cobol to a new era.

While Robert argues extreme viewpoint on both sides, the rest (on my
reading) are scattered in the middle ground between 'don't care either
way', 'keep the status quo', and 'might be nice - but not _those_
symbols'.

> In a worst case scenario, we could be faced with a great 
> division here, with one stream of COBOL language, programs and 
> programmers going one way and another stream going another.  

This of course is a risk when any extremism is allowed to occur.  I
consider the status quo (plus '<>') to suit my needs.  As this is
similar to many other languages that I use (ie and, or, not) then
changing is not required or wanted.

Robert had argued (unsupported) that adding symbols for these would be
less confusing for programmers of other languages, however, the ones
he proposed would have been confusing for _everyone_.  It would, as
you say, be unfortunate if they were made available, and were used, as
it will make a wider split with existing code.

> This sort of thing is not easy to resolve, but I wish the 
> COBOL community the best in doing so.  

The resolution is simple, Robert, or anyone else that wants to, can
use REPLACE or some other pre-processor to use whatever he feels like.
 Unfortunately the argument is not about that, It is about Robert
convincing us about how we _should_ or _must_ be writing our code.  It
is about whether Robert's ego dictates how our programs are written or
whether we should be able to choose based on individual preferences.

As a bit of history Robert came to this group to 'rub our noses in it'
and has been looking for sticks to beat Cobol programmers, and
especially the J4, with. He especially dislikes all of us doing things
the way we prefer rather than the way that Robert has devised.
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3edda5b8.472031892@news.optonline.net...

> You're missing why the standard included the symbols <= and >=. They could
have
> been REPLACEd just as easily.

That is true.  However, the rationale and justification for adding symbolic
abbreviations for synonyms for the negation of a subset of the relational
operators is in my view different from the justification for adding symbolic
abbreviations for the entire class of logical operators (the two connectors
and the negator).

I will also point out (not for the first time!) that, though I was not on
the committee at the time GREATER THAN OR EQUAL TO and LESS THAN OR EQUAL TO
were added as relational operators to the standard (this, together with the
synonyms >= and <= respectively, appear in the '85 standard) I would almost
certainly have argued against doing so.   I would only reluctantly support
the addition of <> to the set of extended relational operators, largely on
the grounds that it completes the set already formed by =, <, >, <= and >=.
If <= and >= weren't already there (much to my chagrin!), I'd see absolutely
no reason to consider adding <>.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-04T08:49:33-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbktdg$lll$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <01c329f9$9f7a6520$64ccf943@chottel> <bbiqfd$2j0g$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
Just for the "record" (information already passed on elsewhere to Chuck).
According to the 2002 Standard

     REPLACE ==^== BY ==AND==, ==!== BY ==NOT==, ==|== BY ==OR==.
        ...
     IF A != B ^ C != D | E ...

would be NON-conforming source code. because you can't have

      ==^==   *> or any of the other symbols in this REPLACE

The rules of the 2002 Standard (and possibly the '85 Standard - I haven't
checked for a reference there) *REQUIRE* that the "text-word" in pseudo-text
be:
  - a literal, or a
 - separator, or a
 - set of contiguous COBOL character

As "^" "!" and "|" are *not* already COBOL characters, they can't be used as
pseudo-text  text words.  There are some "odd" ways around this, but it does
answers Chuck's final,

 " I missing something?"
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-04T13:32:47-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbll1g$1jjp$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <01c329f9$9f7a6520$64ccf943@chottel> <bbiqfd$2j0g$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <bbktdg$lll$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:bbktdg$lll$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net...
> Just for the "record" (information already passed on elsewhere to Chuck).
> According to the 2002 Standard
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> checked for a reference there) *REQUIRE* that the "text-word" in
pseudo-text
> be:
>   - a literal, or a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> As "^" "!" and "|" are *not* already COBOL characters, they can't be used
as
> pseudo-text  text words.

Thanks, Bill.  That pesky "text-word" definition has nailed me before.

But that brings up some interesting points, actually.

None of the three symbols Mr. Wagner suggests is currently in the "basic
special character" set, so any symbols not made up of the characters in the
*current* lists will require additions to it.  I don't agree that this
feature is of sufficient merit to warrant the addition of characters to that
list.

But for the purposes of discussion, let's presume I did.

Mr. Wagner's first choice for a synonym for "AND" was "&", but that's
already used for concatenation and isn't available as an operator.  His
second choice was "^" because it's similar in appearance to the symbol used
in symbolic logic for "AND".

There's a symbol that's closer to the standard symbol for "AND" in that
milieu:  "/\", made up of "/" and "\".  These characters can be reversed to
produce "\/", the standard symbol for "OR".

Adding the backslash to the list would make "/\" a legitimate text-word.  As
a byproduct, it would make "\/" a text-word.  A sequence like "IF A = B /\ C
= D \/ E = F ..." would then be made up of text-words, and all components of
it eligible for REPLACE.

As it happens, I agree with you and others that adding a "universal" symbol
for "NOT" causes additional problems inadequately addressed by the proposal,
and that without having an abbreviation for "NOT" it doesn't make a lot of
sense to have such abbreviations for "AND" and "OR".  But presuming the
contrary view prevailed, a high-school textbook on symbolic logic uses the
tilde, "~", as the symbol for negation.

Given, then, that the goal here is to incorporate symbols familiar to
symbolic logicians into COBOL, it seems to me the most logical choices are
/\ for AND, \/ for OR, and if not vetoed on other grounds, ~ for NOT.  This
has the additional advantage that only two, not three, characters would need
to be added to the "basic special character list", there would be no
particular need for anybody to use them if they didn't want to, and any
programmers wanting to use "standard logical symbols" in COBOL could do
something like
    REPLACE ==/\== by AND, ==\/== by ==OR==, ==~== by ==NOT==
and code statements of the form
    IF A ~= B /\ C ~= D \/ E
or (presuming <> is also added to the standard as elsewhere proposed) the
equivalent
    IF A <> B /\ C <> D \/ E
to their hearts' content.

As I see it, the only significant change to the standard that would be
required to accomplish this would be to add backslash and tilde to the
"basic special character" table on Page 61.  The three symbols incorporated
from symbolic logic -- /\, \/, and ~ -- would then be legitimate
"text-words", and the desired goal accomplished using the REPLACE statement.
Adding symbols for AND and OR (while leaving NOT alone because of the points
already addressed by others) would require only the addition of the
backslash character to the list.

I personally have far fewer defenses against the idea of adding a single
special character (or two) to the table on page 61, absent any other changes
to the standard, than I do against adding symbolic synonyms for AND and OR
(though others on the committee might object more strenuously to the former
addition than I!).

Mr. Wagner is free to steal this idea and propose it to the committee if he
wishes, in an amended version of his document J4/03-0145; I make no
guarantees as to how such an amended document would be received, however.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-06-05T10:08:30+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3edf0f7f.564660768@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <01c329f9$9f7a6520$64ccf943@chottel> <bbiqfd$2j0g$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <bbktdg$lll$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bbll1g$1jjp$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
Jallan suggested /\ and \/  a few days ago. His article cited a Web page whereon
a code set expert and early standards pioneer claiming to be "the father of the
backslash" described why it was introduced to the ASCII & EBCDIC sets -- exactly
for this purpose. 

Jallan's first choice was the Unicode values U+2227, the typographic symbol for
/\, and U+2228 for \/ (along with U+00AC for NOT). He suggested the slash
digraphs as a fallback for those lacking access to Unicode. Personally, I don't
think adding backslash to the COBOL code set is warranted just to provide a
fallback. The words AND and OR are  an adequate fallback. 

What do you think about adding Unicode symbols to the COBOL set? 


"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message

> What do you think about adding Unicode symbols to the COBOL set?
>
I  would be willing to see characters from the (not actually portable)
Latin-1 character set added to the "basic COBOL character repertoire" - I
would NOT be happy with adding other characters from Unicode (or ISO 10646).

It should be noted that the 2002 Standard *does* allow a specific set of
"extended" characters to be ALLOWED by a vendor for "user-defined words".  I
understand the PROs and CONs of this, but would not like to see these
characters expanded to be used in COBOL reserved words.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-05T10:15:56-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbntse$6u3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <01c329f9$9f7a6520$64ccf943@chottel> <bbiqfd$2j0g$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <bbktdg$lll$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bbll1g$1jjp$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edf0f7f.564660768@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3edf0f7f.564660768@news.optonline.net...

> Jallan's first choice was the Unicode values U+2227, the typographic
symbol for
> /\, and U+2228 for \/ (along with U+00AC for NOT). He suggested the slash
> digraphs as a fallback for those lacking access to Unicode. Personally, I
don't
> think adding backslash to the COBOL code set is warranted just to provide
a
> fallback. The words AND and OR are  an adequate fallback.
>
> What do you think about adding Unicode symbols to the COBOL set?

I think that adding individual symbols that happen to be represented in the
Unicode set, "having access to" Unicode, and being able to use the entire
repertoire (or a specific non-COBOL-defined subset) of the Unicode set while
constructing COBOL words are all different things.

The COBOL character set -- that which is used in the syntax of the
language -- is limited to the space, the digits, the upper-case and
lower-case alphabetics, and a VERY limited group of special characters (+ -
* / = $ , ; . " ( ) > < : in the '85 standard; & ' _ were added in 2002),
and each of those special characters has a defined meaning in the syntax of
COBOL or it wouldn't be in the list.  So far as I know, each of these
characters have Unicode representations, but that isn't actually COBOL's
business.

There have been some long (and tumultuous) discussions regarding Unicode,
ISO/IEC 646, ISO/IEC 10646-2, ISO/IEC DTR 10652  and other similar documents
and their relationship with the COBOL standard during J4 and WG4 meetings
(Appendix D of ISO/IEC 1989:2002 is evidence of an effort to resolve
conflicting goals represented by proponents of Unicode and proponents of DTR
10652, for example).  I have assiduously avoided involving myself in that
controversy, but I very definitely prefer that the COBOL standard *not*
specify the character set that COBOL programmers shall use for writing
programs.

That being said, I *think* it'd be a lot easier to get the backslash
(whatever its representation in whichever character set the programmer
happens to be using in the implementation) into the standard's set of basic
special characters than it would the *graphics* -- to say nothing of the
specific encoding -- from Unicode.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

EBCDIC.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Symbols for Logical Operators

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** jallan@smrtytrek.com (jallan)
- **Date:** 2003-06-10T11:10:44-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<299f1138.0306101010.63939574@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bbgn0a$11u7$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0d2e.367429814@news.optonline.net> <01c329f9$9f7a6520$64ccf943@chottel> <bbiqfd$2j0g$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <bbktdg$lll$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bbll1g$1jjp$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edf0f7f.564660768@news.optonline.net> <bbntse$6u3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote in message news:<bbntse$6u3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>...
 
> That being said, I *think* it'd be a lot easier to get the backslash
> (whatever its representation in whichever character set the programmer
> happens to be using in the implementation) into the standard's set of basic
> special characters than it would the *graphics* -- to say nothing of the
> specific encoding -- from Unicode. 

One possible problem: the backslash was not part of the invarient
subset of ASCII and was replaced by other characters in various
national encodings.

Most of these sets are effectively obsolete now, but not all.

In most standard Japanese encodings which use ASCII as the base the
position of the backslash is taken by the Yen sign.

Even Unicode has not put an end to this. Unicode Japanese fonts
shipped with Windows replace the proper backslash with a Yen symbol in
flagrant contradiction to Unicode specifications.

CP 494, the Windows code page for Korea, has the Won sign in place of
the backslash.

There may be other code pages in common use that are similar.

I don't know whether the same substituion of a currency sign for a
backslash occurs in any EBCDIC code pages in common use, whether
officially or unofficially.

Of course, when the symbols < > = were introduced into COBOL in the
beginning, most business computer sites did not support them either,
which is I think the reason why COBOL from the beginning had verbal
fall backs for them.

So similarly today, it might be considered unimportant that some
Japanese environments would not support \ properly, that \/ and /\
would be displayed as ï¿½/ and /ï¿½ resectively. The words OR and AND
would sill be available as fall backs.

Jim Allan
```
